The Hanafi Dispute on Persian in Prayer
Clarifying the ruling on starting prayer and reciting in Persian for those able to speak Arabic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the original positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on starting and reciting in Persian, and identify the retraction reported in later sources.

Core Idea
A person unable to speak Arabic may start prayer in Persian by agreement; the early dispute was resolved through mutual retraction.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's hashia clarifies a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states the Sahibayn's prohibition on starting and reciting in Persian for those able to speak Arabic. However, a transmission from al-Tatarkhaniyya reveals that the Sahibayn later retracted their position on starting, agreeing with Abu Hanifa that it is permissible for the unable. This lesson walks you through the original disagreement, the retraction, and the practical implications for prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage deals with two distinct acts: starting the prayer in Persian and reciting Quranic verses in Persian. The rulings may differ, as indicated by the retraction being one-sided for each.
Starting is the takbir and initial dhikr; recitation is the Quranic verses.
2
State the original positions
According to the matn, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that starting and reciting in Persian are impermissible for someone who can speak Arabic. Abu Hanifa initially permitted starting but not reciting? The passage suggests he held a different view on starting.
The exact positions are reconstructed from the retraction narrative.
3
Analyze the retraction from al-Tatarkhaniyya
The hashia quotes al-Tatarkhaniyya stating that starting in Persian is permissible 'by agreement' for the one who is unable. This indicates that the Sahibayn retracted their prohibition on starting and accepted Abu Hanifa's view.
No retraction is mentioned for recitation; instead, Abu Hanifa retracted to the Sahibayn's view on recitation.
4
Distinguish between unable and able
The retraction applies specifically to the person who is incapable of Arabic. For the able, the original prohibition (makruh tahrimi) remains in effect. The hashia implies that the dispute was resolved only for the 'ajiz.
The term 'ajiz refers to someone unable to learn or pronounce Arabic correctly.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a person who can speak Arabic but starts prayer in Persian would be following a position that is not mufta bihi; the relied-upon view is that it is impermissible (makruh tahrimi). For the unable, it is permissible. Recitation in Persian remains impermissible for all, as Abu Hanifa retracted to the Sahibayn's view.
This shows how later authorities reconciled the conflicting transmissions.

Source Text

صاحبيه في عدم جواز الشروع في الصلاة بالفارسية لغير العاجز عن العربية وعدم جواز القراءة فيها بالفارسية، وغيرها من أي لسان غير عربي لغير العاجز عن العربية، وعدم جواز الاقتصار في السجود على الأنف بلا عذر في الجبهة لحديث أمرت أن أسجد على سبعة أعظم على الجبهة الحديث نقل في الدر عن التتارخانية أنّ الشروع بالفارسية كالتلبية يجوز إتفاقاً أي لغير العاجز فظاهره رجوعهما إليه لا هو إليهما، وهذا عكس القراءة فإنه رجع إليهما.
His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the impermissibility of starting prayer in Persian for one who is not incapable of Arabic, and the impermissibility of recitation in Persian, and any other non-Arabic language for one who is not incapable of Arabic, and the impermissibility of limiting prostration to the nose without an excuse regarding the forehead due to the hadith: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones, on the forehead...' the hadith. It is narrated in al-Durr from al-Tatarkhaniyya that starting in Persian is permissible by agreement, i.e., for the one who is unable, so apparently the two companions retracted to his view, not he to theirs, and this is the opposite of recitation, for he retracted to theirs.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in the hashia. The matn states that according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not permissible for a person who can speak Arabic to start the prayer in Persian, nor to recite in Persian or any other non-Arabic language. However, the hashia notes a transmission from al-Tatarkhaniyya that starting in Persian is actually permissible by agreement (meaning both companions and Abu Hanifa agree) for the person who is unable to recite Arabic. This implies that the two companions have retracted their earlier position and now agree with Abu Hanifa. This is contrasted with the ruling on recitation where Abu Hanifa retracted to the companions' view.

Key Terms
صاحبيه (ṣāḥibayhi): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
الفارسية (al-fārisiyyah): The Persian language, used here as an example of a non-Arabic language for prayer.
العاجز (al-ʿājiz): One who is incapable of reciting Arabic correctly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hashia from al-Tatarkhaniyya, what is the ruling on starting prayer in Persian for someone unable to recite Arabic?
A) It is impermissible (makruh tahrimi).
B) It is permissible by agreement of the two companions and Abu Hanifa.
C) It is invalid (batil).
D) It is recommended (sunnah).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolved the early dispute on Persian in prayer through mutual retraction, allowing starting in Persian for the unable but maintaining prohibition for the able, and keeping recitation in Persian prohibited for the able.

Conditions of Sujud: Height, Hands, Knees, and Feet
Explaining conditions for valid sujud: height limit and placement of hands, knees, and feet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid sujud (height limit, placement of hands and knees, placement of feet) and explain the Hanafi interpretation of the seven-bones hadith.

Core Idea
Prostration requires forehead at most half a cubit above feet; hands and knees are sunnah, not wajib, despite the hadith listing them.

Prostration (sujud) is a pillar of prayer, but its validity depends on specific conditions. This passage from the Hashiya details the limits on the height of the prostration place and the required placement of hands, knees, and feet. It also resolves an apparent conflict between the famous hadith of the seven bones and the Hanafi ruling that hand and knee placement is sunnah, not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
The passage lists three main conditions for valid sujud: (1) the place of prostration (forehead) must not be more than half a cubit above the feet; (2) at least one hand and one knee must be placed on the ground; (3) the inner part of the toes of both feet must touch the ground.
The condition for hands and knees is considered sunnah in the school; the other two are more stringent.
2
Understand the height condition
The height limit is half a cubit (about 45 cm). A slight rise does not harm, but if it exceeds, the sujud is invalid unless repeated before leaving the prayer. An exception is made for extreme crowding when one may prostrate on the back of another person in prayer.
The exception requires the back to belong to a person who is also in prostration and conditions about knees etc.
3
Analyze the hadith and its interpretation
The hadith 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones' seems to obligate placing all seven limbs. The Hanafi answer: the hadith indicates the *place* of sujud, not that all must necessarily touch. The command (amr) primarily applies to the forehead; the other limbs are sunnah based on the school's usul.
This is a key example of how Hanafis interpret amr in a descriptive context, not always as an obligation.
4
Recognize the internal and external differences
Internally, some Hanafi scholars (al-Kamal) considered hand/knee placement wajib, but the chosen view (mufta bihi) is sunnah. Externally, the Shafi'i school considers it wajib based on the same hadith. The practical difference is that omitting a sunnah does not invalidate sujud, but omitting a wajib would require compensation (sajdah al-sahw).
This thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is important for understanding the school's position.
5
Note the ruling for the feet
The passage clearly states that the toes must be placed with the inner side (batin) on the ground, not the outer side. The majority view from Karkhi, Muhit, Quduri holds that placing only one foot is not sufficient; both feet must be used.
The hadith says 'ends of the feet' (atraf al-qadamayn), implying plural.

(And) from the conditions of valid prostration is that the place of prostration does not rise above the place of the feet by more than half a cubit, so that the description of 'prostrator' is realized. A slight rise does not harm. (And if it increases beyond half a cubit, prostration is not valid, i.e., it is not considered valid. If he does another action considered to be correct, his prayer is valid, and if he departs from his prayer without repeating it, it becomes invalid. (Except) if there is crowding, he prostrates on the back of a person in prayer out of necessity. If that back is not of someone in prayer, or is in another prayer, it is not permitted. (And) from the conditions of valid prostration is (placing) one hand (and) one knee in the correct view, as we have mentioned, (and) placing (some of the toes of) the feet, directing the inner side toward the Qibla, (during prostration on the ground). It is not sufficient to place the outer side of the foot, because that is not the place due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones: on the forehead, the hands, the knees, and the ends of the feet' - agreed upon. And this is the choice of the fuqaha. And there is a difference of opinion on permissibility when one places only one foot. His saying: (And the impermissibility of restricting prostration to the nose) you have already known what al-Kamal and the author of al-Hidayah said. His saying: (The hadith 'I have been commanded') the hadith is narrated in many versions, including the narration of al-‘Abbas, in which the face is mentioned instead of the forehead, and it has preceded. His saying: (And a slight rise) what is a slight rise? That which is half a cubit or less. His saying: (on the back of a person in prayer) al-Kifayah stipulates that the knees of the prostrator be on the ground, and al-Mujtaba stipulates that the one being prostrated on is prostrating on the ground. So the total conditions become five, even six with the addition of crowding. However, in al-Qahistani from al-Asl, it is permissible even on the back of a non-praying person. Al-Zahidi transmitted its permissibility on the back of any edible animal. In al-Qahistani from Sadr al-Qudat, it is permissible even if the second prostration is on the back of a third person. In this case, the third prostrator is in the description of a person bowing or more. It is transmitted from al-Jallabi that it is recommended to delay until the crowding clears. End. His saying: (And it is the choice of the fuqaha) and it is said that placing the hands and knees is sunnah. Accordingly, it is said: The hadith requires obligatory prostration on the seven specified limbs, but they did not uphold that. The answer is that the hadith is used to indicate the place of prostration, not that placing all of them is inevitably required. So placing the hands and knees is sunnah in our school. His saying: (And there is a difference of opinion on permissibility) and the apparent view in Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, al-Muhit, and al-Quduri is impermissibility. Al-Zahidi said this, as in the sharh.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by the hashia of the conditions for valid sujud (prostration). It details several conditions: First, the place of prostration (forehead) must not be more than half a cubit above the feet; a slight rise is excused, but if it exceeds, the sujud is not valid unless repeated. However, if necessity due to crowding forces one to prostrate on the back of another person in prayer, it is allowed, provided certain conditions are met (the back is of a prostrating person, etc.). Second, the worshiper must place at least one hand and one knee on the ground; the correct view in the Hanafi school is that placing them is sunnah, not wajib, even though the hadith mentions them. Third, the feet must be placed with the inner part of the toes on the ground, not the outer side, because the hadith specifies 'ends of the feet'. There is a difference of opinion on whether placing both feet is required; the dominant view (from Karkhi, Muhit, Quduri) is impermissibility if only one foot is placed. The hashia also resolves an apparent contradiction: the hadith seems to obligate all seven limbs, but the Hanafi scholars interpret it as indicating the place of sujud, not the obligation of touching all limbs.

Key Terms
السجود (al-sujūd): Prostration: placing the forehead on the ground in a specific manner as part of prayer.
نصف ذراع (niṣf dhirāʿ): Half a cubit: a unit of length, approximately the distance from elbow to fingertips (about 45 cm).
الزحمة (al-zaḥma): Crowding in the mosque, necessitating prostrating on the back of another person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for placing the hands and knees during prostration?
A) It is fard (obligatory).
B) It is sunnah (recommended).
C) It is makruh (disliked).
D) It is haram (invalid).

Key Takeaway
The conditions for valid sujud include a height limit and proper placement of body parts; the Hanafi school interprets the seven-bones hadith as indicating the place of prostration, making hand and knee placement sunnah, not wajib.

The Requirement of Raising from Sujud and the Sequence of Ruku and Sujud
Expansion on conditions of sujud and sequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on raising from sujud to near-sitting and the necessity of ruku before sujud, and distinguish between fard and wajib in this context.

Core Idea
The second prostration is only valid after a near-sitting raise; the order of ruku then sujud is a condition for validity.

This lesson expands on two interconnected rules in salah: the proper sequence of bowing and prostrating, and the requirement to raise from prostration to a near-sitting position before the second prostration. While these may seem like minor details, the rulings have significant implications for the validity of the prayer and when sajdat al-sahw becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rulings
The hashia discusses two conditions: (1) raising from sujud to near-sitting before the second prostration, and (2) performing ruku before sujud. Locate these in the Arabic text and note their connection.
Both are derived from the Prophet's practice and the hadith of the person who prayed badly.
2
Extent of Raising from Sujud
The preferred view (al-asahh) requires raising to a near-sitting position (qurb al-qu'ud), not merely separating the forehead from the ground. Identify the various opinions mentioned and the final tarjih.
Ibn al-Humam and others consider the near-sitting requirement wajib; omitting it necessitates sajdat al-sahw.
3
Sequence: Fard vs Wajib
The hashia clarifies a seeming contradiction: the matn states order is a condition (shart) for validity, yet sajdat al-sahw is prescribed for reversal. Understand the resolution: fardiyyah of order means the second action's validity depends on the first; wujub means violation can be corrected without invalidating the prayer.
This distinction is critical for knowing when the prayer is invalid vs when it can be fixed.
4
Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Qawma and Jalsa
Abu Yusuf holds that qawma (standing from ruku) and jalsa (sitting between sujuds) are fard, making their omission invalidate the prayer. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider them wajib, requiring sajdat al-sahw. Identify the mufta bihi position.
The hashia sides with the majority (wajib) citing Ibn al-Humam and constancy (muwazabah) as proof.

Thus the prostration is realized (i.e., the second prostration), and his saying 'by returning after it' means after nearness to sitting. His saying: (And some scholars mentioned) close to it is what al-Hasan narrated, that if he raises his head as much as the wind passes through, it is permissible. His saying: (And al-Quduri mentioned) some derived from it that if he prostrated on an elevated surface and then it was removed, he prostrated... Commentary on it: (The obligatory recitation) (And) it is required (raising from prostration to near-sitting, according to the most authentic from Imam, because he is considered sitting by his nearness to sitting, so the prostration is realized by returning after it to it, otherwise not. And some scholars mentioned that if he separates his forehead from the ground and then returns it, it is permissible, but no verification is known for him. And al-Quduri mentioned that it is a measure that the name of 'raising' applies, and Shaikh al-Islam considered it more authentic, or what the observer calls 'raising'. (And) it is obligatory (?) to realize the prostration without it, because 'prostrator' is a name for one who places his face on the ground. And it has been narrated that he said: The one who prays while his hair is tied is like the one who prays while his hands are tied. So the similitude indicates negation of perfection, not permissibility, as in al-‘Inayah. His saying: (And the difference of opinion on permissibility) and the apparent in Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, al-Muhit, and al-Quduri is its impermissibility. Al-Zahidi said this, as in the sharh. His saying: (And it is required for the validity of bowing and prostration) its consequence is that if he bows before reciting, or prostrates before bowing, the prayer becomes invalid. And in al-Kafi something indicating this. And in it, concerning sajdat al-sahw, if he introduces a pillar before another, he prostrates for forgetfulness. This would imply obligation of order without its being obligatory (fard), which is contradictory. And the author of Jami‘ al-Fusulayn, the scholar Ibn Qadi Samawah, in Sharh al-Tashil, answered that the meaning of 'fardiyyah of order' is that the validity of the second depends on the existence of the first, such that if one bows after prostration, the prostration is not valid, so he must repeat it. And the meaning of 'wujub' is that violating it does not invalidate the prayer if he repeats it. Mentioned by al-Sayyid. His saying: (there does not remain after it a standing in which the obligatory recitation can be performed) e.g., if he bows in the second rak‘ah of Fajr before reciting, and does not recite after the raising, the prayer becomes invalid. But if he leaves recitation in the first two rak‘ahs of a four-rak‘ah prayer and performs it in the last two, it is valid because there is a standing after this standing in which the obligatory recitation can be performed. And also, if he recites after the raising from bowing in the previous case, it becomes valid if he repeats the bowing because it was nullified by the recitation after it. So reflect. His saying: (And it is required raising from prostration) al-Sayyid transmitted from al-‘Allamah Miskin that the standing from bowing and the sitting between the two prostrations are fard according to Abu Yusuf, so if one omits the standing or the sitting, his prayer is invalid according to him, contrary to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). As for tranquility (tuma'ninah) in the sitting between the two prostrations, it is wajib. The author in his hashia on al-Durar, citing al-Bahr, stated: what the evidence necessitates is obligation of tranquility in the four (i.e., in bowing, prostration, standing, and sitting), and obligation of the very act of raising from bowing and sitting between the two prostrations due to constancy on all that, and due to the command in the hadith of the person who prayed badly, and due to what Qadikhan mentioned of the necessity of prostration for forgetfulness when omitting raising from bowing forgetfully. Similarly in al-Muhit. Thus the ruling for sitting between prostrations is the same because the speech regarding both is one. The opinion of obligation of all is the choice of Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam and his student Ibn Amir Haj, to the extent that he said it is correct. And its completion is there. His saying: (because he is considered sitting by his nearness to sitting because what approximates something gives its ruling).
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by the hashia on two issues: the sequence of ruku and sujud, and the requirement of raising from prostration to a near-sitting position before the second prostration. The matn states that it is a condition to raise from sujud to near-sitting according to the most authentic opinion from Imam Abu Hanifa, because the person is considered sitting when close to sitting, and the second prostration is realized by returning to it from that near-sitting. The hashia then discusses various opinions: some scholars say that merely separating the forehead from the ground and returning is sufficient; al-Quduri said the raising must be such that the name 'raising' applies. On the sequence, the hashia explains a contradiction: the matn seems to require the order (ruku before sujud) as a condition of validity, yet sajdat al-sahw is prescribed for reversing them, which suggests wujub (obligation) rather than fard (essential). The resolution by Ibn Qadi Samawah is that the fardiyyah means the second action's validity depends on the first, so if one prostrates before bowing, the prostration is invalid and must be repeated; wujub means that failure to maintain order can be remedied without invalidating the entire prayer if repeated. The hashia also discusses the opinion of Abu Yusuf that the standing from bowing (qawma) and sitting between prostrations (jalsa) are fard, while for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad they are wajib. The preferred view according to Ibn al-Humam and others is that all these acts (including tranquility) are wajib, and omitting them requires sajdat al-sahw.

Key Terms
الرفع من السجود (al-raf‘ min al-sujūd): Raising oneself from prostration to a sitting position before the second prostration.
القومة (al-qawma): The standing up from bowing (ruku) before prostrating.
الجلسة بين السجدتين (al-jalsa bayna al-sajdatayn): The sitting posture between the two prostrations.
الطمأنينة (al-ṭuma'nīna): Tranquility and stillness in the relevant postures of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (mufta bihi), what is the ruling for omitting the sitting between the two prostrations (jalsa) in prayer?
A) The prayer becomes invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid but one must perform sajdat al-sahw.
C) The prayer is valid without any compensation.
D) The prayer is nullified and the person has sinned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi prayer, raising from sujud to near-sitting and maintaining the sequence of ruku before sujud are wajib acts; their omission requires sajdat al-sahw but does not invalidate the prayer.

Condition of Rising from Sujud
Defining the minimum required lift from sujud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum required lift from sujud according to the relied-upon Hanafi view and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its ruling (fard vs. wajib).

Core Idea
Lifting the head to near sitting is wajib; mere separation is not enough.

In this lesson, we examine the condition of lifting from prostration (raf' min as-sujud) in the Hanafi school. The text from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi refines the minimum required degree of lift and clarifies an intra-Hanafi dispute: whether the lifting (and the sitting between sajdahs) is fard or wajib. Understanding this distinction is critical for determining the validity of prayer and the need for sajdah sahw.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on minimum lift
Identify the relied-upon view: lifting to near sitting (qurb al-qu'ud) is required. Mere separation (muzayalah) is insufficient.
The text says 'the most sound narration from Imam Abu Hanifa' requires near sitting.
2
Identify variant views within the school
Some mashayikh and al-Quduri considered mere separation sufficient. Note that al-Tahtawi rejects this as uncorrected.
These are weak or rejected positions; the mufta bihi is near sitting.
3
Analyze the dispute on ruling (fard vs. wajib)
Abu Yusuf considers the standing from ruku' and sitting between sajdahs as fard. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider them wajib. The lift from sujud follows the same pattern.
The text reports Abu Yusuf's view from 'Allamah Miskin.
4
Examine the evidence
The definition of 'sajid' (one who places face on ground) implies lifting breaks the state. The hadith of twisted hair indicates imperfection, not prohibition. The hadith of the 'musi' salatuhu' commands tuma'ninah, implying wujub.
This is qiyas khafi, linking to the definition and prophetic practice.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that lifting to near sitting is wajib. If omitted forgetfully, sajdah sahw is required. The prayer is valid but incomplete.
Ibn al-Humann and his student preferred this as 'the correct view'.

أعادها جازت ولم يعلم له تصحيح وذكر القدوري أنه قدر ما ينطلق عليه اسم الرفع وجعله شيخ الإسلام أصح أو ما يسميه الناظر رافعاً (و) يفترض لتحقق السجدة بدونها لأن الساجد إسم لمن وضع الوجه على الأرض، وقد روي أنه قال : مثل الذي يصلي، وهو عاقص شعره كمثل الذي يصلي، وهو مكتوف فالتمثيل يدل على نفي الكمال لا الجواز كما في العناية. قوله : (واختلف في الجواز) وظاهر ما في مختصر الكرخي، والمحيط والقدوري عدم الجواز. قاله الزاهدي كذا في الشرح قوله: (ويشترط لصحة الركوع والسجود الخ) مقتضاه أنه إذا ركع قبل أن يقرأ، أو سجد قبل أن يركع فسدت، وفي الكافي ما يفيده، وفيه من سجود السهو لو قدم ركناً عن ركن سجد للسهو، وهذا يقتضي وجوب رعاية الترتيب دون فرضيته، وفيه تناقض، وأجاب صاحب جامع الفصولين العلامة ابن قاضي سماوة في شرح التسهيل بأن معنى فرضية الترتيب توقف صحة الثاني على وجود الأول حتى لو ركع بعد السجود لا يكون السجود معتداً : به فيلزمه إعادته ومعنى وجوبه أن الإخلال به لا يفسد الصلاة إذا أعاده ذكره السيد قوله : (لم يبق بعده قيام يصح به فرض القراءة) كما إذا ركع في ثانية الفجر قبل القراءة، ولم يقرأ بعد الرفع فإنها تفسد أما إذا ترك القراءة في الأوليين من الرباعية، وأداها في الأخيرتين صحت لوجود قيام بعد هذا القيام يصح فيه فرض القراءة، وكما إذا قرأ بعد الرفع من الركوع في الصورة السابقة فإنها تصح إذا أعاد الركوع لأنه إنتقض بوجود القراءة ،بعده، فليتأمل قوله : ( ويشترط الرفع من السجود الخ) نقل السيد في العلامة مسكين أن القومة من الركوع والجلسة بين السجدتين فرضان عند أبي يوسف و مقتضاه أنه لو ترك القومة، أو الجلسة فسدت صلاته عنده خلافاً لهما وأما الطمأنينة في الجلسة بين السجدتين فواجبة، وذكر المصنف في حاشية الدرر معزياً للبحر ما نصه، ومقتضى الدليل وجوب الطمأنينة في الأربعة أي في الركوع والسجود، وفي القومة، والجلسة، ووجوب نفس الرفع من الركوع والجلوس بين السجدتين للمواظبة على ذلك كله، وللأمر به في حديث المسيء صلاته ولما ذكره قاضيخان من لزوم سجود السهو بترك الرفع من الركوع ساهياً، وكذا في المحيط، فيكون حكم الجلسة بين السجدتين كذلك لأن الكلام فيهما واحد، والقول بوجوب الكل هو مختار المحقق ابن الهمام، وتلميذه ابن أمير حاج حتى قال أنه الصواب، وتمامه فيه. قوله: (لأنه يعد جالساً بقربه من القعود لأن ما قارب الشيء يعطي حكمه قوله: (فتحقق (السجدة) أي الثانية، وقوله بالعود بعده أي بعد القرب من القعود. قوله: (وذكر بعض المشايخ الخ) يقرب منه ما رواه الحسن أنه إذا رفع رأسه بقدر ما تمر فيه الريح جاز. قوله : (وذكر القدوري فرع بعضهم عليه أنه لو سجد على مرتفع فأزيل فسجد
The recitation is obligatory. (And) the lifting from prostration to near sitting is a condition according to the most sound narration from Imam Abu Hanifa, because one is considered sitting by being near to sitting, so the prostration is realized by returning after that to it; otherwise not. Some mashayikh mentioned that if he removes his forehead from the ground and then returns it, it is permissible, but no correction is known for him. Al-Quduri mentioned that it is the amount that the name of lifting applies, and Shaykh al-Islam considered it more correct: or what an observer would call a lifter. And it is obligatory for the realization of the prostration without it, because 'the one who prostrates' is the name for one who places his face on the ground, and it has been narrated that he said: 'The example of one who prays while his hair is twisted is like the example of one who prays while his hands are tied' – the analogy indicates negation of perfection, not permissibility, as in al-Inaya. He said: (and there is disagreement on permissibility) and the apparent in Mukhtasar al-Karkhi, al-Muhit, and al-Quduri is non-permissibility, as stated by al-Zahidi, likewise in the commentary. He said: (and it is a condition for the validity of bowing and prostration etc.) – its implication is that if one bows before reciting, or prostrates before bowing, the prayer is invalidated. In al-Kafi there is what indicates that, and in it regarding forgetfulness prostrations: if one brings a pillar before another pillar, he performs prostration for forgetfulness. This requires that order is obligatory (wajib) but not a fard. There is contradiction in this. The author of Jami' al-Fusulayn, the scholar Ibn Qadi Samawiyya, in Sharh al-Tashil responded that the meaning of the fardiyya of order is that the validity of the second depends on the existence of the first, such that if one bows after prostration, the prostration is not counted, so he must repeat it. The meaning of its wujub is that failing it does not invalidate the prayer if he repeats it, as mentioned by al-Sayyid. He said: (and after it there remains no standing with which the fard of recitation can be performed) as if he bows in the second rak'ah of Fajr before recitation and does not recite after lifting, then it is invalidated. But if he omits recitation in the first two of a four-rak'ah prayer and performs it in the last two, it is valid because there is standing after this standing in which the fard of recitation is possible. And likewise if he recites after lifting from bowing in the previous scenario, it is valid if he repeats the bowing because it is invalidated by the recitation after it. So let him reflect. He said: (and it is a condition to lift from prostration etc.) – al-Sayyid reported from Alamah Miskin that the standing from bowing and the sitting between the two prostrations are fard according to Abu Yusuf, and its implication is that if one omits the standing or the sitting, his prayer is invalid according to him, contrary to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). As for tranquility in the sitting between the two prostrations, it is obligatory (wajib). The author mentioned in Hashiyat al-Durar, quoting al-Bahr, the text: 'And the requirement of evidence is the obligation of tranquility in the four: bowing, prostration, standing, and sitting, and the obligation of the lifting itself from bowing and sitting between the two prostrations due to persistence on all that, and due to the command in the hadith of the one who prayed badly, and because Qadikhan mentioned the necessity of prostration of forgetfulness for omitting the lifting from bowing forgetfully, and similarly in al-Muhit. So the ruling of the sitting between the two prostrations is likewise because the talk about both is one. And the opinion of obligation of all is the choice of al-Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humann, and his student Ibn Amir Hajj, even saying that it is correct. And its completion is in it.' He said: (because he is considered sitting by being near to sitting because whatever is near to a thing takes its ruling). He said: (so the prostration is realized) meaning the second, and his saying 'by returning after it' means after nearness to sitting. He said: (and some mashayikh mentioned) close to it is what Hasan narrated: that if he raises his head to the extent that wind passes through, it is permissible. He said: (and al-Quduri mentioned) some based on it that if he prostrates on an elevated place and then it is removed, so he prostrates.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi is a critical refinement of the conditions for prostration (sujud) in prayer. It discusses the requirement of lifting the forehead from the ground (raf') after each sajdah. The author states that according to the most authentic narration from Imam Abu Hanifa, one must lift until he is nearly sitting (qurb al-qu'ud). Merely removing the forehead (muzayalah) is insufficient; one must approach the sitting posture. There is a dispute: some early scholars (mashayikh) said merely separating the forehead suffices, but al-Tahtawi notes this correction is not accepted. Al-Quduri defined the minimum as what can be called 'lifting', and Shaykh al-Islam (likely Abu Hanifa's student) considered the 'near sitting' view more correct. The author also discusses whether order (tartib) between actions is fard (invalidating) or wajib (requiring repetition with sajdah sahw). He resolves the apparent contradiction by explaining: if order is fard, omission invalidates the prayer; if wajib, the prayer is valid but must be repeated with sajdah sahw. The relied-upon view, per Ibn Qadi Samawiyya, is that order is fard in the sense that the second action depends on the first, but its omission does not invalidate the entire prayer if corrected. The passage also reports that Abu Yusuf considers the standing from ruku' and the sitting between sajdahs as fard, unlike Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The author concludes that tranquility (tuma'ninah) in all four pillars (ruku', sujud, standing after ruku', sitting between sajdahs) is wajib, and the lifting itself is also wajib, supported by the hadith of the 'musi' salatuhu' (the one who prayed badly) and the ruling on sajdah sahw for omitting lifting. The preferred view (mukhtar) of Ibn al-Humann and his student is that all these are obligatory (wajib), not fard.

Key Terms
الرفع من السجود (ar-raf' min as-sujud): Lifting the forehead and other limbs from the ground after the first prostration, to a state where one is nearly sitting.
المزايلة (al-muzayalah): The act of separating the forehead from the place of prostration, without necessarily rising to a sitting posture.
قرب القعود (qurb al-qu'ud): The state of being near to sitting, i.e., raising the head until one is almost in a seated position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum amount of lifting required after the first sajdah according to the most authentic Hanafi view?
A) Merely separating the forehead from the ground
B) Lifting until one is nearly sitting (qurb al-qu'ud)
C) Lifting the hands as well
D) Standing up completely

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view requires lifting to near sitting (qurb al-qu'ud) after each sajdah; mere separation is insufficient, and the lift is wajib, not fard.

The Obligation of the Final Sitting in Prayer
Parsing the ruling on the final sitting in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) and explain why it is considered fard but not an original pillar.

Core Idea
The final sitting is fard, not a primary pillar—its omission requires sajdah sahw, not repetition.

In this lesson, we examine the legal status of the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) in prayer. While all schools agree it is obligatory, the Hanafi school uniquely classifies it as a condition (shart) rather than an original pillar (rukn asli). This classification has practical implications for how unintentional omissions are handled.

Guided Path
1
Define the final sitting and its scope
Identify what the final sitting is: the sitting at the end of the prayer for tashahhud. Note that it applies to all prayers, even those without a first sitting (e.g., Fajr, Jumu'ah, traveler's prayer).
This clarifies that the ruling covers all obligatory prayers.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its classification
The final sitting is fard (obligatory). However, the Hanafi school debates its classification: some call it an original pillar (rukn asli), but the relied-upon view (in al-Bada'i') is that it is not an original pillar. It is either an additional pillar (rukn za'id) or a condition (shart). The author leans toward it being a condition.
The difference in classification affects the consequence of omission.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference of opinion
Yusuf ibn 'Asim considered it an original pillar. The preponderant view (sahih) is that it is not. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school, with the mufta bihi being the latter view.
Knowing both views is important for fatwa practice.
4
Derive the evidence from the oath example
The text cites an example: if someone swears 'I will not pray' and then performs a rak'ah with prostration (i.e., completes the essential actions), he does not break his oath because the final sitting is not included in the basic definition of 'prayer' for oath purposes. This indicates that the final sitting is not a primary pillar but a condition or additional requirement.
This is a qawl (saying) used as evidence for the classification.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Consider cases: If one intentionally omits the final sitting, the prayer is invalid. If omitted forgetfully, sajdah sahw is required. This distinguishes it from original pillars, whose omission always invalidates.
This step ties the classification to concrete fiqh outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويفترض القعود الأخير (الخ) أي الذي يقع آخر الصلاة وإن لم يتقدمه أول فشمل الصبح والجمعة وصلاة المسافر، واختلف فيه فقيل ركن من الأركان الأصلية وإليه مال يوسف بن عاصم وفي البدائع الصحيح أنه ليس بركن أصلي ومفهومه أنه ركن زائد، وهو خلاف الظاهر، والظاهر أنه شرط لقولهم : لو حلف لا يصلي، فقيد الركعة بالسجدة يحنث،
(And the final sitting is obligatory (etc.) i.e., which occurs at the end of the prayer even if it is not preceded by a first, so it includes Fajr, Jumu'ah, and the traveler's prayer. And there is difference of opinion about it: some said it is a pillar from the original pillars (arkan asliyyah), and Yusuf ibn 'Asim inclined to that. In al-Bada'i', the correct view is that it is not an original pillar, and its implication is that it is an additional pillar (rukn za'id), which is contrary to the apparent. And the apparent is that it is a condition (shart), because of their statement: if one swears not to pray, and he restricts the rak'ah to the prostration, he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage parses the ruling on the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) in prayer. The author clarifies that this is the sitting that occurs at the very end of the prayer, even for prayers like Fajr, Jumu'ah, or traveler's prayer that have only two rak'ahs (where there is no first sitting). The scholars differ: some consider it an original pillar (rukn asli) like the other pillars; Yusuf ibn 'Asim favored that view. However, the correct view in al-Bada'i' is that it is not an original pillar; it is either an additional pillar (rukn za'id) or a condition (shart). The author leans toward it being a condition, based on the example: if someone swears 'I will not pray' and then performs a rak'ah with prostration (i.e., completes a full unit of prayer), does he break the oath? The answer: the final sitting is not included in the basic definition of 'prayer' for the purpose of the oath; thus the oath only refers to the essential elements (the rukn and sujud). This indicates that the final sitting is not considered a primary pillar but a condition or separate requirement. The passage is brief and associated with the larger discussion in the text.

Key Terms
القعود الأخير (al-qu'ud al-akhir): The final sitting at the end of the prayer, for reciting tashahhud and salawat.
الركن الأصلي (ar-rukn al-asli): An original pillar of prayer, such as qiyam, ruku', sujud. The final sitting is not considered an original pillar in the Hanafi school according to the relied-upon view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) according to the relied-upon Hanafi view?
A) It is sunnah mu'akkadah
B) It is an original pillar (rukn asli)
C) It is fard but not an original pillar
D) It is optional

Key Takeaway
The final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) is fard in Hanafi fiqh but is not an original pillar; it is classified as a condition (shart) or additional pillar, and omitting it intentionally invalidates the prayer.

The Ruling and Condition of Returning to the Second Prostration
Explains ruling, evidence, and wisdom of returning to second sajdah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of returning to the second prostration and identify the correct condition for its validity according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The second prostration is fard; raising the head and hands suffices, not all seven limbs.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the ruling of returning to the second prostration in prayer. It clarifies that while the second prostration is obligatory by consensus, the precise manner of transitioning—particularly which limbs must be lifted—has been a point of refinement within the Hanafi school. The commentator uses hadith evidence and internal critique to settle on the most correct view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed and its legal status. The act is 'returning to the second prostration' (al-‘awd ila al-sujūd al-thānī). The ruling is that it is fard (obligatory) by consensus of the Ummah.
The commentator uses 'fard' and 'farḍ' interchangeably, but in Hanafi terminology it is fard because it is a pillar of prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited to support the ruling. The passage cites two hadiths: 'Pray as you have seen me pray' and 'Indeed the hands prostrate as the face prostrates.' These are from the Sunnah.
The hadith are ahad (single-transmitter), so their thubut is zanni.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Determine how the hadiths prove the ruling. The command 'sallu' is an imperative (amr), which generally indicates obligation. However, the commentator interprets it here as recommendation (nadb) because the specific action of raising hands is shown in the Prophet's practice. The second hadith analogizes hands to face in prostration, implying they should be raised when the face is raised.
The commentator argues that the hadith only indicates raising the head and hands, not all seven limbs.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the differing views within the Hanafi school. Some narrations require moving all seven limbs (forehead, two hands, two knees, two feet) between prostrations. The correct (asahh) position is that raising the head and hands suffices.
Shaykh al-Islam (al-Haskafi) considered the view of minimal movement more correct.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the operative ruling for fatwa. The relied-upon position is that raising the head and hands from the ground and then placing them is sufficient; moving all seven limbs is sunnah but not required for validity.
This view is preferred by later Hanafi authorities al-Tahtawi and al-Haskafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العود إلى السجود الثاني لأن السجود الثاني كالأول فرض باجماع الأمة ولا يتحقق كونه كالأول إلا بوضع الأعضاء السبعة ولا يوجد التكرار إلا بعد مزايلتها مكانها في السجود الأول فيلزمه رفعها، ثم وضعها ليوجد التكرار وبه وردت السنة كان صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سجد ورفع رأسه من السجدة الأولى رفع يديه من الأرض، ووضعهما على فخذيه، وقال : صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي، وقال : إن اليدين تسجدان كما يسجد الوجه فإذا وضع أحدكم وجهه فليضعهما، وإذا رفعه فليرفعهما، وحكمة تكرار السجود قيل تعبدي وقيل ترغيماً للشيطان حيث لم يسجد ،مرة، وقيل لما أمر الله بني آدم بالسجود عند أخذ الميثاق، ورفع المسلمون رؤسهم ونظر، والكفار لم يسجدوا خروا سجداً ثانياً شكراً لنعمة التوفيق وامتثال ثانياً، بلا رفع أصلاً صح عن الثانية، وفيه تأمل قوله : (وجعله شيخ الإسلام أصح) أي في أداء الفرض وأن تحققت معه الكراهة. قوله : (أو ما يسميه الناظر رافعاً) هذه رواية رابعة عن الإمام وقد علمت الأصح قوله: (ويفترض العود إلى السجود) منه يعلم أن مراده بقوله أولاً السجود السجدة الواحدة لا الجنس كما قدمناه قوله : ( ولا يتحقق كونه كالأول إلا بوضع الأعضاء السبعة) أي لا يتحقق كونه فرضاً كالأول إلا الخ وفيه نظر فإن الفرضية كما قدمه تتحقق بوضع الجبهة وإحدى اليدين والركبتين وشيء من أطراف أصابع القدمين قوله : (إلا بعد مزايلتها مكانها في السجود فيه نظر فإن الأصح كما قدمه إشتراط الرفع إلى قرب القعود وأما المزايلة فلم يصحح الإكتفاء بها أحد، وكلامه يفيد أنه لا بد من مزايلة الأعضاء السبعة مكانها، وهو ليس بشرط إلا في الجبهة قوله : (وبه وردت السنة) أي بالرفع، ثم الوضع مسلم أن هذا هو السنة، وليس ركناً والدليل قاصر على إفادة رفع الرأس، واليدين ووضعهما، وهو المطلوب، ولا يفيد مزايلة جميع أعضاء السجود كما ذكر قوله : (صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي) لا شك في حمل الأمر هنا على الندب، وهو غير الدّعي قوله: (قيل تعبدي) أي تعبدنا به الحق تعالى، ولم نطلع على حكمته، كعدد الركعات فنفعله كما أمرنا، ولا نطلب في المعنى . قوله : (وقيل ترغيماً للشيطان وقيل : لما سجد الملائكة لآدم عليه السلام حين أمروا بالسجود له، ولم يسجد إبليس فانقلب وجهه إلى ظهره، وظهر عليه شعور كشعور الخنازير، فسجد الملائكة ثانياً شكر لتوفيق الله تعالى إياهم، فأمرنا بالسجدتين متابعة لهم، وقيل الأولى لشكر الإيمان، والثانية لبقائه وقبل الأولى إشارة إلى أنه خلق من الأرض، فالثانية إشارة إلى أنه يعاد فيها.
Returning to the second prostration because the second prostration is like the first, obligatory by consensus of the Ummah. Its being like the first is only realized by placing the seven limbs. Repetition only occurs after moving them from their place in the first prostration, so raising them is necessary, then placing them for repetition. The Sunnah has been transmitted with this. He (peace be upon him) when he prostrated and raised his head from the first prostration, he raised his hands from the ground and placed them on his thighs, and said: 'Pray as you have seen me pray.' And he said: 'Indeed the hands prostrate as the face prostrates. So when one of you places his face, let him place them, and when he raises it, let him raise them.' The wisdom of repeating the prostration is said to be devotional, and said to be for humiliating Satan, as he did not prostrate once. And it is said: When Allah commanded the children of Adam to prostrate at the taking of the covenant, the Muslims raised their heads and looked, while the disbelievers did not prostrate; they fell in prostration a second time as thanks for the grace of success and obedience a second time. Without raising at all, it is correct from the second, but there is consideration. His saying: (And the Shaykh al-Islam considered it more correct), meaning in performing the obligation even if dislike occurs with it. His saying: (Or what the observer considers raising), this is a fourth narration from the Imam and you have known the most correct. His saying: (And it is obligatory to return to prostration), from this it is known that his meaning by 'first prostration' is the single sajdah, not the genus, as we have mentioned. His saying: (It is not realized as being like the first except by placing the seven limbs), meaning it is not realized as an obligation like the first except... etc., but there is consideration. For the obligation, as he mentioned, is realized by placing the forehead, one of the hands, the knees, and something from the tips of the toes. His saying: (Except after moving them from their place in prostration) has consideration. For the most correct, as he mentioned, is the condition of raising to near sitting, but as for moving, none have declared it sufficient. His saying: (And with it the Sunnah has been transmitted) meaning with raising, then placing. It is accepted that this is the Sunnah, not a pillar. And the evidence is limited to indicating raising the head and hands and placing them, which is what is required, and it does not indicate moving all the limbs of prostration as he mentioned. His saying: (Pray as you have seen me pray), there is no doubt that the command here is interpreted as recommendation, and it is not a claim. His saying: (It is said devotional) meaning we are devoted to it by Allah, and we have not known its wisdom, like the number of rak'ahs, so we do it as commanded, and we do not seek the meaning. His saying: (And it is said: for humiliating Satan. And it is said: When the angels prostrated to Adam (peace be upon him) when they were commanded to prostrate to him, and Iblis did not prostrate, so his face turned to his back, and hair appeared on him like the hair of pigs. So the angels prostrated a second time as thanks for Allah's grace upon them. So we were commanded to perform two prostrations following them. And it is said: the first for thanks for faith, and the second for its continuation. And it is said: the first is an indication that he was created from earth, and the second is an indication that he will return to it.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof and commentary on the necessity of returning to the second prostration (sajdah) in prayer. The commentator (al-Tahtawi) explains that the second prostration is obligatory by consensus, just like the first. To achieve the repetition, the worshipper must fully raise the seven limbs (forehead, two hands, two knees, two feet tips) from the ground between the two prostrations, then place them again. The evidence comes from the sunnah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) raised his hands off the ground on rising from the first prostration and placed them on his thighs, and commanded, 'Pray as you have seen me pray,' and also said the hands prostrate like the face. The passage then discusses various wisdoms behind the two prostrations: a devotional mystery, humiliation of Satan, following the angels' example, and symbolic references to creation and return to earth. The commentator refines the matn by clarifying that the 'seven limbs' condition is not a strict requirement for validity except for the forehead, and that the hadith evidence only supports raising the head and hands, not all seven limbs. He also notes that the command in the hadith is for recommendation, not obligation, but the overall ruling of returning to the second prostration is obligatory as per the consensus.

Key Terms
السجود (al-sujūd): The act of prostration in prayer; here specifically the second prostration in each rak'ah.
الأعضاء السبعة (al-a‘ḍā’ al-sab‘ah): The seven body parts that must touch the ground during prostration: forehead, two hands, two knees, two feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the condition for the second prostration to be valid?
A) Moving all seven limbs away from their place in the first prostration.
B) Raising the face and hands off the ground and then placing them again.
C) Reciting the tashahhud between the two prostrations.
D) Having the intention for the second prostration.

Key Takeaway
The second prostration is fard; the correct Hanafi view is that raising the head and hands between prostration suffices, not all seven limbs.

Obligation of the Final Sitting in Prayer
Establishes obligation of final sitting and refutes its status as a pillar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the final sitting, identify its proof, and explain why it is considered wajib and not a rukn.

Core Idea
The final sitting is wajib, not a rukn, because prayer's essence does not depend on it.

The final sitting (qa‘dah akhīrah) in prayer is a key component whose legal status differs across schools. In this passage, the commentator al-Tahtawi establishes the Hanafi view that it is an obligation (wajib) rather than a pillar (rukn), citing the hadith of Ibn Mas‘ud and addressing internal and external disputes. Understanding this distinction is vital for knowing when a prayer is invalid versus when it can be corrected through sajda sahw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (the final sitting) and its hukm level (wajib). Note the consensus and the exception of Malik.
The commentator begins by stating it is obligatory by consensus, except Malik who considers it sunnah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith of Ibn Mas‘ud where the Prophet (peace be upon him) made completion of prayer dependent on the tashahhud.
The hadith is a single transmission (khabar wahid), so it is zanni in both thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the obligation: the Prophet said 'when you say this or do this, you have completed your prayer' – the completion is tied to the sitting and tashahhud. The Hanafi interpretation is that the choice ('if you wish to stand or sit') applies to the tashahhud recitation, not the sitting itself.
The commentator clarifies that the 'choice' does not negate obligation; it refers to staying or leaving after obligatory recitation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the dispute: Malik considers it sunnah, Shafi'i considers it a rukn. Also intra-Hanafi: some scholars considered it a condition or a rukn, but the mu'tamad is wajib.
The commentator refutes the notion of it being a rukn by arguing that prayer does not depend on it (e.g., traveler can omit it without invalidating).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi position prevails: the sitting is prescribed for exiting prayer, not for itself; it is for rest; the reality of prayer is complete with standing, bowing, and prostration.
Al-Tahtawi cites al-Siraj, al-Bahr, al-Nahr to support that it is not a pillar.

Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof and critical commentary on the obligation of the final sitting (qa‘dah akhirah) in prayer. The commentator (al-Tahtawi) begins by asserting that the final sitting is obligatory by scholarly consensus, except for Imam Malik who considered it sunnah. The Hanafi position is that the obligatory component is sitting for the duration of reciting the tashahhud, based on the hadith of Ibn Mas‘ud where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'When you say this (the tashahhud) or do this, you have completed your prayer.' The commentator explains that the sitting must come after the pillars (ruku‘, sujud) because it is prescribed to conclude them. It is also a condition that all actions be performed while awake; if one performs an action while asleep, it is invalid. However, there is a dispute about the final sitting: according to Munyat al-Musalli, one must repeat the sitting if done while asleep, but according to Jami‘ al-Fatawa, it is counted because sitting is not a pillar but merely for rest. The commentator notes this as a practical outcome of the disagreement on whether the final sitting is a condition or a pillar. He then argues that the final sitting is not a pillar because the essence of prayer does not depend on it; prayer exists without it (like the case of a traveler shortening prayer). The sitting is prescribed for exiting prayer, while pillars are prescribed for themselves. He also clarifies that the hadith's phrase 'if you wish to stand or sit' indicates choice regarding the tashahhud recitation, not the sitting itself. He adds that if a missed prostration (sajdah salbiyyah) or tilawah prostration is remembered after the final sitting, it is repeated after the sitting; but prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdah sahw) do not require repeating the sitting. Finally, he discusses the condition of having knowledge of the prayer's obligatory attributes, meaning one must believe in the obligation of the prayer's components.

Key Terms
القعود الأخير (al-qa‘dah al-akhīrah): The final sitting in the last rak'ah of prayer, during which the tashahhud is recited.
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited in the final sitting: 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi... ashhadu an la ilaha illallah wa ashhadu anna Muhammadan 'abduhu wa rasuluh.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The fact that the Prophet always performed it.
D) The verse of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The final sitting is wajib (not a rukn) because the reality of prayer does not depend on it; it is a condition for completion and its omission requires sajda sahw.

The Final Sitting in Prayer: Fard Status and Proof
Establishes fardiyyah of final sitting with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the final sitting, identify the hadith proof, and distinguish between the Hanafi and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
The final sitting is fard as a condition for prayer, proved by the hadith tying completion to tashahhud.

In every prayer, after the last prostration, the worshiper sits and recites the tashahhud. But what is the legal status of that sitting? Is it a pillar that invalidates the prayer if missed, a condition, or just a recommended practice? This lesson examines the Hanafi position: the final sitting is fard (obligatory) as a condition, supported by a clear hadith and distinguished from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Source
Read the passage and note that the final sitting is declared fard by consensus, with the proof from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud. The phrase 'if you say this, you have completed your prayer' links completion to the sitting.
The consensus includes all schools except Malik, who considers it sunnah.
2
Analyze the Proof Structure
The hadith uses a conditional: completion of prayer is tied to the sitting (which includes tashahhud). The principle 'what is indispensable for an obligation is itself obligatory' makes the sitting fard.
The hadith is ahad (single chain), but its meaning (dalala) is definitive (qati).
3
Classify the Nature of the Sitting
The passage clarifies that the final sitting is a condition (shart), not a pillar (rukn). This means if it is omitted, the prayer is invalid, but it does not require repetition like a missing pillar.
Some Hanafis argue it is a rukn, but the relied-upon view is that it is a shart.
4
Examine the Maliki Difference
Imam Malik holds the final sitting is sunnah. The Hanafi response is that the hadith establishes obligation, and the Maliki view weakens the consensus claim but does not affect Hanafi proof.
The practical difference: for Malik, omitting the sitting does not invalidate the prayer; for Hanafis, it does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر (و) يفترض القعود الأخير باجماع العلماء وإن اختلفوا في قدره والمفروض عندنا الجلوس (قدر) قراءة (التشهد في الأصح لحديث ابن مسعود رضي الله تعالى عنه حين علمه التشهد : إذا قلت هذا أو فعلت هذا فقد قضيت صلاتك إن شئت أن تقم فقم وإن شئت أن تقعد فاقعد علق تمام الصلاة به وما لا يتم الفرض إلا به فهو فرض وزعم بعض مشايخنا أن المفروض في القعدة ما يأتي فيه بكلمة الشهادتين فكان فرضاً عملياً (و) يشترط (تأخيره) أي القعود الأخير عن الأركان) لأنه شرع لختمها فيعاد لسجدة صلبية تذكرها قوله : (بإجماع العلماء إلا مالكاً فإنه روي عنه أنه سنة. قوله: (إذا قلت هذا أي التشهد أي وأنت قاعد فإنه لم يعهد تشهد إلا في قعود. قوله : (أن تقم) أن مصدرية، ولعل الرواية بإثبات الواو (۱) إذ لا وجه لحذفها . قوله : (وإن شئت الخ) لعله منسوخ أو التخيير بالنظر لما سنذكره قوله : (علق) تمام الصلاة به أي بالقعود لأنه لا يتخير فيه وإنما التخيير في التلفظ بالتشهد ومعنى التخيير عدم توقف الماهية عليه، وإن كان واجباً. قوله : (فيعاد السجدة صلبية) مثلها التلاوية لا السهوية، فإنها ترفع التشهد لا القعود قوله: (تذكرها) أي بعد القعود، ولو بعد السلام قبل الكلام كما في الدر
The command: The final sitting is fard by consensus of the scholars, though they differed over its measure. According to us, the fard is sitting for the duration of reciting the tashahhud in the soundest view, due to the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) when he taught him the tashahhud: 'When you say this or do this, you have completed your prayer. If you wish to stand, then stand, and if you wish to sit, then sit.' He tied the completion of prayer to it, and that without which the fard is not fulfilled is itself fard. Some of our shaykhs held that the fard in the sitting is what contains the word of the two testimonies, so it was a practical fard. And it is required to delay it (i.e., the final sitting) after the pillars, because it is prescribed for concluding them, so a structural prostration remembered after it is repeated. He said: 'By consensus of the scholars, except Malik, for it is narrated from him that it is sunnah.' He said: 'When you say this' i.e., the tashahhud, i.e., while sitting, for tashahhud is not known except in sitting. He said: 'that you stand' — 'an' is masdariyyah, and perhaps the narration is with the waw (1) since there is no reason to omit it. He said: 'And if you wish, etc.' — perhaps it is abrogated, or choice with consideration to what we will mention. He said: 'He tied' — completion of the prayer to it, i.e., to the sitting, because he does not choose in it, rather the choice is in uttering the tashahhud, and the meaning of choice is that the essence is not dependent on it, even if it is obligatory. He said: 'So a structural prostration' — like it is the recitational prostration, not the prostration of forgetfulness, for the latter removes the tashahhud, not the sitting. He said: 'if he remembers it' i.e., after the sitting, even after the salutation before speech, as in al-Durr.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a hāshiya (critical refinement) on Maraqi al-Falah, addressing the final sitting (qa'dah akhirah) in prayer. The base text states that the final sitting is fard (obligatory) by ijma', though scholars differ on its measure. The preferred Hanafi view is that sitting for the duration of reciting the tashahhud is fard. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud where the Prophet (peace be upon him) linked the completion of prayer to this action, and the principle that what is indispensable for an obligation becomes obligatory. The commentator notes that Imam Malik holds it to be sunnah, contradicting the consensus claim. The condition of delaying the final sitting after the pillars is then discussed, with the example of a structural prostration (such as from forgetfulness) that must be performed after the final sitting if it was missed. The commentator also clarifies that the 'choice' in the hadith refers to the tashahhud utterance, not the sitting itself. The intra-Hanafi difference on whether the final sitting is a rukn or shart is introduced, with the preferred view being that it is a condition (shart).

Key Terms
القعود الأخير (al-qa'dah al-akhirah): The final sitting in prayer after the second sajdah of the last rak'ah, during which tashahhud is recited.
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited in the final sitting, including 'at-tahiyyatu lillahi...'
الصلبية (al-sulbiyyah): A prostration of forgetfulness that is structural (related to a missed pillar or obligation), distinguished from the recitational prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary proof that the final sitting in prayer is fard in the Hanafi school?
A) Consensus (ijma') of all scholars
B) The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud
C) Analogy (qiyas) to the standing in prayer
D) The principle that any action of the Prophet is obligatory

Key Takeaway
The final sitting is fard in the Hanafi school as a condition for prayer, proven by the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, and it is not a pillar (rukn) but a condition (shart).

The Condition of Wakefulness in Prayer and the Final Sitting While Asleep
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the final sitting during sleep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi views on the final sitting performed while asleep and state the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
Sleep invalidates rukn actions in prayer, but the final sitting's status as condition or pillar determines its validity.

Prayer requires wakefulness for all its actions. But what if one falls asleep during the final sitting? This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars say the prayer is invalid and must be repeated, while others deem it valid. The difference stems from whether the final sitting is a condition (shart) or a pillar (rukn). Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage begins by stating a condition: all pillars and other acts must be performed while awake. Note that sleep nullifies bowing, standing, and prostration.
2
Recognize the Exception
The final sitting is treated differently. The text says there is a dispute (khilaf) about it. Identify the two cited sources: Munyat al-Musalli and Jami' al-Fatawa.
3
State View 1 and Its Basis
Munyat al-Musalli holds that if the final sitting is performed asleep and not repeated, the prayer is invalid. This view considers the final sitting a condition that requires wakefulness.
4
State View 2 and Its Basis
Jami' al-Fatawa holds that the prayer is valid because the final sitting is not a pillar (rukn) but is based on rest (istirahah), which is compatible with sleep.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if it is a condition, sleep invalidates and repetition is required; if it is not a condition, sleep does not affect validity. The text does not prefer one view, but notes the root of disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و (يشترط لصحة الأركان وغيرها أداؤها مستيقظاً) فإذا ركع أو قام أو سجد نائماً لم يعتد به وإن طرأ فيه النوم صح بما قبله منه وفي القعدة الأخيرة خلاف قال في منية المصلي إذا لم يعدها بطلت وفي جامع الفتاوى يعتد بها نائماً لأنها ليست بركن ومبناها على الاستراحة فيلائمها النوم قلت وهو ثمرة الاختلاف في شرطيتها وركنيتها قوله : (وغيرها) ظاهره يعم الواجبات، والسنن والآداب فلا يعتد بها إلا بالإختيار ويعم الشرائط، وفيه أن القعدة الأخيرة على القول بشرطيتها لا يشترط لها الإستيقاظ كما ذكره بعد قوله : (أو قام) وكذا لو قرأ على الأصح
And it is a condition for the validity of the pillars and other acts that they be performed while awake. So if one bows, or stands, or prostrates while asleep, it is not counted. But if sleep overtakes him during an act, what preceded it is valid. Regarding the final sitting, there is a dispute. In Munyat al-Musalli it says: if he does not repeat it, the prayer is invalid. In Jami' al-Fatawa, it is deemed valid while asleep because it is not a pillar, and its basis is rest, so sleep is compatible with it. I say: this is the fruit of the disagreement over whether it is a condition or a pillar. He said: '(and others) — its apparent meaning includes the obligatory, sunnah, and etiquette acts, so they are not counted except with choice; and it includes the conditions. In this, the final sitting according to the view that it is a condition does not require wakefulness, as he mentioned later: '(or stood)' and likewise if he recited, according to the soundest view.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) addressing the condition that all actions of prayer must be performed while awake. If one performs ruku', qiyam, or sujud while asleep, those actions are invalid and must be repeated. For the final sitting, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: one view (from Munyat al-Musalli) holds that if the final sitting is performed asleep and not repeated, the prayer is invalid; another view (from Jami' al-Fatawa) considers it valid because the final sitting is not a pillar but a condition based on rest, and sleep is compatible with rest. The commentator then notes that the difference stems from whether the final sitting is a condition or a pillar. The commentary on 'and others' clarifies that this condition of wakefulness applies to all obligatory, sunnah, and adab actions, but for the final sitting specifically, the condition view does not require wakefulness, contrary to other actions.

Key Terms
اليقظة (al-yaqazah): Wakefulness; being conscious and aware during prayer acts.
النوم (al-nawm): Sleep; the state that nullifies consciousness and choice.
الاستراحة (al-istirahah): Rest; the nature of the final sitting as a restful pause rather than an active pillar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference of opinion regarding the final sitting if performed while asleep?
A) Some say it must be repeated, others say it is valid
B) All agree it is invalid
C) All agree it is valid
D) The ruling differs based on the type of sleep

Key Takeaway
The final sitting in prayer is subject to an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of sleep, with the key being whether it is considered a condition or a rest.

Condition of Knowledge and Pillars of Prayer
Critical refinement on conditions of prayer and listing of pillars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of knowing the prayer's manner and identify the agreed-upon pillars of prayer in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
To pray validly, you must distinguish fard from sunnah; the agreed pillars are four: standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration.

This passage refines the requirement of knowing the prayer's manner. While some might think detailed knowledge of every attribute is needed, the Hanafi school clarifies that a general belief in the fard/sunnah distinction suffices. The passage also lists the four agreed-upon pillars, noting that other elements like the final sitting and opening takbīr are disputed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for valid prayer
Locate the phrase 'و يشترط لصحة أداء المفروض إما (معرفة كيفية)' which establishes knowledge of the prayer's manner as a condition.
This condition is about belief, not action.
2
Understand the scope of knowledge required
The passage explains that the worshiper must distinguish fard attributes from sunnah attributes, or simply believe that the entire prayer is fard.
Detailed knowledge of each sunnah act (like thanā') is not required.
3
Note the principle that nafl can be fulfilled by fard intention
The text states that a supererogatory prayer is fulfilled by intending it as fard, but the reverse does not hold.
This underscores the necessity of correct intention and belief.
4
List the agreed-upon pillars
The passage explicitly says the pillars agreed upon are four: standing (qiyām), recitation (qirā'ah), bowing (rukū'), and prostration (sujūd).
These are the integral parts without which the prayer is invalid.
5
Recognize the disputed elements
It mentions that some consider the final sitting and the opening takbīr (taḥrīmah) as pillars, but the agreed position is that they are conditions.
The 'fruit of the dispute' affects whether the prayer is invalidated if omitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و يشترط لصحة أداء المفروض إما (معرفة كيفية) يعني صفة الصلاة و ذلك بمعرفة حقيقة (ما فيها) أي ما في جملة الصلوات من الخصال) أي الصفات الفرضية يعني كونها فرضاً فيعتقد افتراض ركعتي الفجر وأربع الظهر وهكذا باقي الصلوات (المفروضة) فيكون ذلك على وجه (يميزها الخصال) أي الصفات (المسنونة) كالسنن الرواتب وغيرها باعتقاد سنية ما قبل الظهر وما بعده، وهكذا، وليس المراد ولا الشرط أن يميز ما اشتملت عليه صلاة الصبح من الفرض، والسنة مثل اعتقاد فرضية القيام وسنة الثناء والتسبيح (أو اعتقاد) المصلي (أنها أي أن ذات الصلوات التي يفعلها كلها (فرض) كاعتقاده أن الأربع في الفجر فرض ويصلي كل ركعتين بانفرادهما، ويأتي بثلاث ثم ركعتين في المغرب معتقداً فرضية الخمس (حتى لا يتنفل بمفروض) لأنّ النفل يتأدى بنية الفرض أما الفرض فلا يتأدى بنية النفل كما في التجنيس والمزيد والخلاصة، ثم نبه على الأركان وغيرها فقال : ( والأركان) المتفق عليها (من المذكورات التي علمتها فيما قدمناه بأكثر من سبعة وعشرين (أربعة) وهي (القيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود وقيل القعود الأخير مقدار التشهد) ركن أيضاً، وقيل: شرط وقد بينا ثمرة الخلاف فيه وقيل التحريمة ركن أيضاً (وباقيها) أي المذكورات شرائط بعضها شرط لصحة الشروع في الصلاة وهو ما كان خارجها وهو الطهارة من الحدث والخبث وستر العورة واستقبال القبلة، والوقت
And it is a condition for the validity of performing the obligatory (prayer) either (knowledge of the manner) meaning the description of the prayer, and that is by knowing the reality of what is in it, i.e. what the totality of prayers contains of attributes, i.e. the obligatory attributes, meaning that it is fard. So he believes the fardiyyah of the two rak'ahs of Fajr, and four of Zuhr, and so on for the rest of the obligatory prayers. So that is in a way that (distinguishes them from the attributes) i.e. the sunnah attributes, like the regular sunnah prayers and others, by believing the sunnah nature of what is before Zuhr and after it, and so on. And the meaning is not, nor is the condition, that he distinguish what the Fajr prayer contains of fard and sunnah, like believing the fardiyyah of standing and the sunnah of the praise and glorification. Or (the worshiper's belief) that (the very prayers that he performs are all fard), like his belief that the four (rak'ahs) in Fajr are fard, and he prays each two rak'ahs separately, and he performs three then two rak'ahs in Maghrib, believing the fardiyyah of the five. (So that he does not perform a supererogatory (nafl) prayer with a fard intention) because the nafl is fulfilled by the intention of fard, while the fard is not fulfilled by the intention of nafl, as in al-Tajnīs, al-Mazīd, and al-Khulāṣa. Then he pointed out the pillars and others and said: (The pillars) that are agreed upon (from the mentioned items that you learned in what we presented — numbering more than twenty-seven — are four), namely: (standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration. And it is said that the final sitting to the extent of the tashahhud is also a pillar, and it is said: a condition, and we have shown the fruit of the dispute in it. And it is said that the Taḥrīmah (the opening takbir) is also a pillar. (And the rest) of the mentioned items are conditions. Some of them are conditions for the validity of commencing the prayer, which are those external to it: purity from hadath (impurity) and khabath (filth), covering the 'awrah, facing the qiblah, and (entering) the time.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the conditions of prayer. The base text states that one must have knowledge of the prayer's manner, meaning one must distinguish the obligatory parts from the sunnah. The commentator clarifies that this does not require detailed knowledge of each specific sunnah act like the recitation of thanā' or tasbīḥ, but rather a general awareness of which prayers are fard and which are sunnah. An alternative is to simply believe that all prayers performed are fard, which suffices. The principle is that nafl (supererogatory prayer) can be fulfilled by intending fard, but fard cannot be fulfilled by intending nafl. The passage then lists the agreed-upon pillars of prayer: standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration. Disputed additions include the final sitting (said to be a pillar by some, a condition by others) and the opening takbīr (said to be a pillar by some). The rest of the items (purity, covering 'awrah, facing qiblah, time) are conditions for the validity of starting prayer.

Key Terms
معرفة كيفية (ma'rifat al-kayfiyyah): Knowledge of the manner of prayer, specifically distinguishing fard attributes from sunnah attributes.
الأركان (al-arkan): The pillars of prayer; the integral parts without which the prayer is invalid.
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbīr (Allahu Akbar) to commence prayer; some consider it a pillar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many pillars (arkan) of prayer are mentioned in the passage as agreed upon?
A) Four (standing, recitation, bowing, prostration)
B) Five (adding final sitting)
C) Six (adding takbir tahrimah)
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
To pray validly, one must distinguish fard from sunnah in general belief; the agreed pillars are standing, recitation, bowing, and prostration.
